(Getty Images)","SynopsisShort":"(Getty Images)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/2l/rj/p02lrjlp.jpg","Title":"pregnant_getty_976.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p02lrjlp","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p02lrjlp","_id":"559a555d0b808738c4e2d9a7"},{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":92834,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/2l/rj/p02lrj8n.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"(Thinkstock)","SynopsisMedium":"(Thinkstock)","SynopsisShort":"(Thinkstock)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/2l/rj/p02lrj8n.jpg","Title":"bandage_thinkstock_976.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p02lrj8n","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p02lrj8n","_id":"559a555d0b808738c4e2d9a5"}],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Claudia is a writer, broadcaster and lecturer in\npsychology. She presents &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p002vsyw\"&gt;Health\nCheck&lt;/a&gt; on BBC World Service every Wednesday and her new book is titled Time Warped:\nUnlocking the Mysteries of Time Perception. &lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;If there are myths youd like Claudia to bust in future\ncolumns, shes on Twitter &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/claudiahammond\"&gt;@claudiahammond&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Claudia Hammond","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"e77239ca-7991-40c7-92ab-5793973c26a4","Id":"wwfuture/author/claudia-hammond","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"claudia-hammond"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/claudia-hammond","_id":"559a524c0b808738c4e25f4b"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If you visit someone with a newborn baby, their description of the pain of childbirth often includes the phrase &ldquo;never again&rdquo;.&nbsp; One friend even begged me to dissuade her if she ever suggested having a second child in the future. Yet a few years later there she was, approaching the due date for the next baby. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The explanation usually given is that women simply forget exactly what the pain was like because the brain edits out the memory. In evolutionary terms it might make sense to forget the pain of childbirth in case it deterred people from reproduction. But unfortunately it seems not to be that simple. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;On the subject of childbirth, for instance, a review of studies conducted before 2000, concluded that women do not completely forget labour pain &ndash; or &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11251509/\"&gt;the intensity of the pain they experienced&lt;/a&gt;. It&rsquo;s a difficult topic to study, though. There are scales that allow people to rate their experience of pain, but since childbirth tends to take many hours, it&rsquo;s not always clear which part of labour or childbirth study participants focus on. Even individual contractions vary in their painfulness, so scoring the pain of the whole experience is never going to be easy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02lrj58\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What you can do, though, is examine whether individual women&rsquo;s recollections of their pain change with time. A Swedish study took more than 2,000 mothers and &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/14992155\"&gt;compared ratings of their memories of the pain and experience of childbirth two months after the birth with their ratings 12 months later&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Again, the evidence suggests that most women don&rsquo;t forget: the memory of the experience of childbirth was the same for 60% of the women at two months and 12 months. In fact, just one-third of the mothers at 12 months had forgotten quite how painful they&rsquo;d considered childbirth at two months. And for 18% the opposite happened &ndash; far from forgetting the pain, in their minds the experience had become more painful by 12 months.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Rewriting the past&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Five years later, the researchers talked to the women again. By now, the memory of labour pain had indeed declined in some women &ndash; about half of them rated the pain as less severe than they had done at two months &ndash; but for those who had rated the experience most negatively after two months, &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19076128/\"&gt;the memory of the pain simply didn&rsquo;t go away&lt;/a&gt;. This isn&rsquo;t necessarily a bad thing, though. Some studies have found that women who remember the pain of childbirth see coping with the experience as an achievement that stays with them. &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11251509/\"&gt;If I can cope with labour pain I can cope with anything&lt;/a&gt;.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But it still might come as a surprise that the memory of pain often remains unchanged with time. That&rsquo;s because decades of psychological research on memory show that we reconstruct memories slightly differently every time we recall them. Our memories are not like a DVD library which we can browse through, replaying any past event exactly as it happened &ndash; instead, the context of the original memory and the way we tell the story each time makes a difference. The pain of childbirth is pain with a purpose &ndash; provided the baby is delivered safely, this positive result should, in theory, lessen the memory of the pain. But clearly, for some mothers even the happy event of a new-born baby can&rsquo;t diminish the painful memory of childbirth.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02lrjlp\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So if the intensity of this kind of &ldquo;positive&rdquo; pain isn&rsquo;t necessarily forgotten, how about more negative experiences?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Sometimes it&rsquo;s important that we feel some pain and remember what caused it &ndash; the memory can keep us safe and prevent an accident from happening again. A painful cut gained when opening a tin can, for instance, will generally ensure that you are more careful the next time you&rsquo;re preparing dinner. Or if, like me, you find yourself wondering whether the metal ring around a fire pit gets really hot and you touch it with all four fingers, you probably won&rsquo;t do that again. Sadly that was aged 30, not aged five. As you might expect, then, people with disorders which prevent them from feeling pain at all frequently burn and injure themselves. So we don&rsquo;t necessarily want to forget all pain.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Forgetting some pain might be useful, though &ndash; particularly the chronic pain sometimes associated with conditions like diabetes. Clues to controlling and eliminating this sort of pain at the molecular level come from recent work.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02lrj8n\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Just walking past the doorway where you stubbed your toe, or using the door you once shut your finger in, can make you wince at the memory. In 2006, researchers at the University of Arizona College of Medicine claimed this is because of a molecule called PKMzeta, which they argued strengthens the connections between neurons in the brain when we learn something new &ndash; and also creates physical sensitivity after a painful experience. Using mice, they demonstrated that, if they blocked this specific molecule in the spine, they could erase the extra sensitivity to pain in the mice. However, other researchers have since questioned this finding, showing that &lt;a href=\"http://phenomena.nationalgeographic.com/2013/01/04/deleting-a-memory-molecule-doesnt-affect-memory-in-mice/\"&gt;mice born without PKMzeta could still feel pain&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What about the uncomfortable or traumatic pain that can be associated with some medical procedures? When colonoscopies are done under conscious sedation, patients are sometimes given a drug called midazolam which makes them feel less anxious and also promotes anterograde amnesia &ndash; the inability to form new memories. Patients might wince or wriggle with discomfort during the procedure, but the hope is that they won&rsquo;t remember any pain or feel disturbed by the procedure afterwards.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Lingering feelings&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some have questioned the ethics of this approach &ndash; and its implications. In a paper published last year Andrew Davidson, an anaesthetist at the Royal Children&rsquo;s Hospital in Victoria, Australia, warned that although drugs such as midazolam can make it harder to form conscious memories, &lt;a href=\"http://jme.bmj.com/content/40/10/659.extract\"&gt;they can leave implicit memories intact&lt;/a&gt;. These are memories that affect us even though we can&rsquo;t consciously recall them. So we can button a shirt or find our way along a familiar route to the station without thinking about when we actually made those memories. Troublingly, Davidson suggests that even if you don't remember a colonoscopy, you might feel &ldquo;strangely uncomfortable when walking past a garden hose&rdquo;. Would it be more ethical if patients were warned in advance that this might happen?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s even been suggested that to mitigate the psychological trauma of the very rare situations where patients wake up while under a general anaesthetic, they could be given midazolam as soon as the anaesthetist realises, &lt;a href=\"http://jme.bmj.com/content/40/10/651.short?g=w_jme_top10_tab\"&gt;to disrupt the formation of memories of the event&lt;/a&gt;. But this would only erase memories from the point when the drug was administered, not from the point of waking.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The ethics of the use of drugs like this will continue to be discussed. The very fact that they are being considered underlines the fact that &ndash; unfortunately &ndash; for people who have given birth or experienced other kinds of agony, it can be a myth that pain has no memory.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Share this story on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/bbc_future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Update: This article has been amended to reflect the evidence questioning the importance of the molecule PKMzeta.&nbsp;&nbsp; &lt;br /&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if you don't remember a colonoscopy, you might feel strangely uncomfortable when walking past a garden hose&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"Urban myths and old wives tales under the microscope. Medical broadcaster Claudia Hammond busts misconceptions and untruths in the world of health.","Name":"Medical Myths","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Medical Myths","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:19:10.028675Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"9b03dd37-a6f4-4504-86c5-ccd6c3586046","Id":"wwfuture/column/medical-myths","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:19:10.028675Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/medical-myths"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:19:10.028675Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"9b03dd37-a6f4-4504-86c5-ccd6c3586046","Id":"wwfuture/column/medical-myths","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:19:10.028675Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/medical-myths"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/medical-myths","_id":"559a52610b808738c4e262a6"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262db"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dc"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2015-03-12T09:03:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Why painful memories linger with us","HeadlineShort":"Do we forget painful memories?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"If we didnt forget pain, women would never go through childbirth twice  or so says the myth. But the truth is that agonising memories dont always diminish.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;I&rsquo;ve lost count of the number of people who have told me how they rushed to get everything done in the run-up to their holiday, only to be struck down with a stinking cold the moment they stopped work. It doesn&rsquo;t need to be during wintertime: summer holidays, beach trips and short breaks can all fall foul of a post-work health slump too.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There&rsquo;s even a name for this affliction &ndash; leisure sickness. Ad Vingerhoets, the Dutch psychologist who came up with the name, concedes this diagnosis is not yet established in the medical literature; nevertheless, the idea that you fall ill as soon as you stop work is one which many will recognise from bitter experience. So, how valid is it really?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02k4ljg\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There&rsquo;s been no large systematic study to find out whether people are more likely to fall ill when on holiday than during everyday life. But to try to put some figures on it, Vingerhoets asked more than 1,800 people whether they thought they suffered from leisure sickness. Just over &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/12411765\"&gt;3% of people said they did&lt;/a&gt;. Considering how likely we are to feel stressed and work extra-hard before a holiday, this suggests it&rsquo;s not the norm and even quite unusual &ndash; especially because this study relied on asking people to judge for themselves whether it happened, and we&rsquo;re more likely to remember a holiday ruined by an illness, than all the ones unblemished by a cold. We don&rsquo;t remark on it when we go on holiday and feel fine. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But if a small percentage of people are convinced they experience this, is there a physiological explanation? Almost half the people &lt;a href=\"http://hrcak.srce.hr/20385?lang=en\"&gt;in Vingerhoets&rsquo; study&lt;/a&gt; put it down to the transition from work to holiday. There are several theories why this might be the case.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sick list&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;The first goes that when we finally get the chance to relax, the stress hormones that help us cope with work deadlines get out of balance, and leave us open to infection. Adrenaline helps us to cope with stress, as well as boosting the immune system to help fight infections and keep us well. Along with it comes cortisol which also helps with the stress, but at a cost to the immune system. It all sounds plausible, particularly if the transition from stress to relaxation is fast, but not enough research has been done yet to confirm whether this hypothesis holds true.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There again, perhaps people are ill all along. They are so busy and determined to battle on until their holiday arrives that they simply don&rsquo;t notice they are becoming ill until they have the chance to relax on holiday.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s certainly the case that how we rate our symptoms depends on what else we&rsquo;re doing. The psychologist James Pennebaker found that the less that&rsquo;s going on around a person, the worse they say their symptoms are.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02k4m4t\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He had a set of students rate every 30 seconds of a film to say &lt;a href=\"http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1207/s15324834basp0101_6#.VLAfmNBFAy8\"&gt;how interesting it was&lt;/a&gt;. Then he showed the same film to another group of students and monitored how often they coughed. The more interesting the scene in the film, the less they coughed. In the boring bits they seemed to notice their sore throats and cough a lot&hellip; observations that might interest any concert performers fed up with audiences coughing. That said, while you may notice aches and pains more when you are free from the workplace, you&rsquo;re probably going to notice a thick head and a runny nose however hard you&rsquo;re working.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cabin fever&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;An entirely different possibility is that it&rsquo;s not the stress of work that&rsquo;s making us ill, but the holiday itself. Travelling is always tiring, particularly by plane, and the longer the flight the more likely you are to pick up a virus. The average American gets 2.5 colds a year, and on this basis researchers calculated the chances of catching a cold on a single flight should be &lt;a href=\"http://www.cieh.org/library/knowledge/.../jehrvol3iss1-commoncoldaircraft.pdf\"&gt;1% for an adult&lt;/a&gt;. But when patients were surveyed a week after flying from San Francisco Bay to Denver, 20% of them had developed a cold. If this infection rate held all year round, you&rsquo;d expect to get more than 56 colds a year, in other words be prone to the sniffles every week.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02k4mh1\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Recycling air in planes is often blamed for this rate of infection, but in this study it made no difference. The researchers blamed two other culprits: sitting in an enclosed space, closer to other people&rsquo;s germs than you normally would be; and humidity. They hypothesise that the dry air in planes could cause the virus and bacteria-trapping mucus in our noses to become too thick for the fine hairs to pass it down the throat to our stomachs for destruction.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Vingerhoets is open to other explanations for people getting ill on holiday. There&rsquo;s even a suggestion that if you don&rsquo;t really like a holiday, being ill gets you out of it. But the lack of research in this area makes it impossible to pick out one explanation from the others, and it could even be a combination of these factors.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The good news, though, is that it&rsquo;s rarer than you might expect. Even better, as we get older our immune systems have had more time to develop antibodies ,and we get fewer and fewer colds, whether we&rsquo;re on holiday or not. Finally, that&rsquo;s one aspect of getting older to look forward to.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Share this story on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/bbc_future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2015-02-17T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Why we may fall ill when were on holiday","HeadlineShort":"Why you fall ill on holiday","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Does rushing to meet work deadlines before a break leave you more prone to coming down with an illness? Claudia Hammond finds out.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Does rushing to meet work deadlines before a break leave you more prone to coming down with an illness? Claudia Hammond finds out.","SummaryShort":"How likely is that post-work health slump? ","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-02-17T08:49:19Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"94137a41-3fd0-418c-85f3-d41512d30290","Id":"wwfuture/story/20150216-the-truth-about-holiday-illnesses","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-02-17T08:49:19Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20150216-the-truth-about-holiday-illnesses"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20150216-the-truth-about-holiday-illnesses","_id":"559a564b0b808738c4e2fade"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Many people buy diet drinks and sweeteners in a bid to reduce the amount of sugar they consume. Over the years concerns about their safety have been raised, but how much evidence is there that they&rsquo;re bad for us?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Aspartame is probably the best-known artificial sweetener and the one that&rsquo;s had the worst press. It&rsquo;s an alternative to sugar, created from aspartic acid and phenylalanine. In 1996 a paper suggested that the increase in brain tumours &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/8939194\"&gt;might be linked to a rise in the popularity of aspartame&lt;/a&gt;. Fears continued, and other types of cancer began to be mentioned. There was so much concern that a vast study of almost half a million people was conducted by the US National Cancer Institute &lt;a href=\"http://dietandhealth.cancer.gov/docs/aspartame.pdf\"&gt;and published in 2006&lt;/a&gt;. It found no increase in the risk of brain cancer, leukaemia or lymphoma in people who consumed aspartame.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Likewise when the European Food Safety Authority conducted the most comprehensive review of the evidence to date, they concluded that &lt;a href=\"http://www.efsa.europa.eu/en/efsajournal/doc/3496.pdf\"&gt;at the recommended levels&lt;/a&gt; (40mg a day per kilogram of body weight) it was safe, even for children and pregnant women.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tooth protector&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The reason it doesn&rsquo;t seem to have caused problems is that so little aspartame enters our blood system; it&rsquo;s quickly broken down into by-products. But there is one exception. People with a rare genetic disorder called phenylketonuria or PKU can&rsquo;t break down phenylalanine, one of aspartame&rsquo;s by-products  for them, it isn&rsquo;t safe. This is why sweeteners for people to add at the table or to hot drinks are clearly labelled with a warning that they contain a source of phenylalanine.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some sweeteners do come with side-effects, however. If you&rsquo;ve ever eaten too many sugar-free mints containing xylitol then you might have experienced this for yourself. Xylitol is a type of carbohydrate made from birch and other hardwood trees. It contains 30% fewer calories than sugar and doesn&rsquo;t seem to leave an aftertaste, but if you consume large amounts, it can cause water retention, resulting in diarrhoea. But there is evidence exciting some dentists that it can help prevent tooth decay. Chewing gum or mints sweetened with xylitol has been show to &lt;a href=\"http://www.efsa.europa.eu/en/efsajournal/doc/2266.pdf\"&gt;neutralise plaque acidity on the teeth&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02hqch1\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The newest kid on the block is a sweetener that comes from the stevia plant, except that it isn&rsquo;t really that new. In Paraguay and Brazil stevia has been used medicinally for centuries. It&rsquo;s calorie-free and 300 times sweeter than sugar. In Japan it&rsquo;s been sold as a sweetener for more than four decades. Native to tropical and sub-tropical areas of South and Latin America, sweet compounds called steviol glycosides are extracted from the leaves of the plant by steeping them in water. These compounds pass through the body without being absorbed. Stevia was approved at a food additive in the US in 2008 and in Europe in 2011. The downside is that some find it has a bitter aniseed-like aftertaste and for this reason it&rsquo;s often mixed with artificial sweeteners.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But are steviol glysocides safe? The European Food Safety Authority thinks so after they conducted an analysis of all the available evidence done on &lt;a href=\"http://www.efsa.europa.eu/en/topics/topic/sweeteners.htm#efsa_activities\"&gt;both humans and animals in 2010&lt;/a&gt;. They concluded these compounds don&rsquo;t cause cancer, are not toxic and are safe for children and pregnant women.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The great hope is that it helps people to satisfy their sweet tooth without putting on weight or developing diabetes, yet sweeteners have been available for some time and don&rsquo;t seem to have halted the obesity crisis so far. Scientists still don&rsquo;t know whether there are consequences when the brain registers the sweet taste, but then doesn&rsquo;t get the hit of sugar it expects. The worry is that the sweetness might somehow trick the body into releasing too much insulin, something which could lead to weight gain in the long run. It&rsquo;s not possible to say yet whether this is happening. But the European Food Safety Authority has concluded there&rsquo;s not enough evidence to say that stevia helps to achieve or maintain a healthy body weight.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Glucose intolerance&lt;/strong&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Sweeteners don&rsquo;t seem to deserve their poor reputation, it would appear. But just as it seemed that the evidence was clear, this year some curious new research from Israel was published, suggesting that far from helping to prevent Type 2 diabetes, the artificial sweeteners aspartame, saccharin and sucralose might contribute to it. Healthy mice were given drinking water with one of &lt;a href=\"http://www.nature.com/nature/journal/v514/n7521/full/nature13793.html\"&gt;these three sweeteners added to it&lt;/a&gt;. When they measured their blood glucose levels they found that the mice who had consumed sweeteners were showing glucose intolerance, something associated with Type 2 diabetes, while the mice which drank plain water or water containing ordinary sugar didn&rsquo;t. The reason appeared to lie in the bacteria found in the gut; if they had consumed sweeteners their gut bacteria was changed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Results from mice can&rsquo;t always be extrapolated to people and the usual diet of a mouse is of course very different from ours. But for the second part of their study the researchers used humans, feeding them the maximum daily allowance of saccharin, the equivalent of 40 cans of a typical diet drink. Within five days just over half the people showed some intolerance to glucose. Intriguingly those that did had different gut bacteria.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02hqc7k\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then the researchers took it a step further, carrying out faecal transplants where faeces are passed from one person or animal to another. In this case they took the faeces from the humans and put them in the mice. The humans with glucose intolerance passed it on to the mice, suggesting these bacteria in the gut are the key. But before we decide that sweeteners are harmful after all, this is just a single study conducted mainly on mice, with just seven humans taking part. Even the researchers concede that more research is needed. This study tells us nothing about the long-term impact of sweeteners in humans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2013 a vast study covering more than 300,000 people in eight European countries found no link between the development of Type 2 diabetes and eating or drinking artificial sweeteners.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There is one lesson from all these studies and that&rsquo;s that there is no one category of &ldquo;sweeteners&rdquo; which are good or bad. Each is very different and needs to be researched and considered separately. Considering all this evidence, it seems to be far too soon to reach for the sugary drinks instead.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Share this story on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span style=\"text-decoration: underline;\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span style=\"text-decoration: underline;\"&gt;Google+&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/bbc_future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span style=\"text-decoration: underline;\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Disclaimer&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;All content within this column is provided for general information only, and should not be treated as a substitute for the medical advice of your own doctor or any other health care professional. Always consult your own GP if you're in any way concerned about your health.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2015-01-27T09:42:52Z","HeadlineLong":"Are sweeteners really bad for us?","HeadlineShort":"Are sweeteners bad for us? ","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The food industry relies on alternatives to sugar for a vast range of diet foods. But are they safe to consume? Claudia Hammond investigates.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"The food industry relies on alternatives to sugar for a vast range of diet foods. But are they safe to consume? Claudia Hammond investigates.","SummaryShort":"The truth about sugar-free substitutes","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-01-27T09:42:52Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"a48f8c8c-bd29-4ab2-af26-1bedd95434d0","Id":"wwfuture/story/20150127-are-sweeteners-really-bad-for-us","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-01-27T09:42:52Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20150127-are-sweeteners-really-bad-for-us"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20150127-are-sweeteners-really-bad-for-us","_id":"559a564a0b808738c4e2fab0"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Whatever form it takes, an appalling ordeal like a car crash or a natural disaster can have a significant and long-lasting impact on the human condition. One common assumption is that everyone is prone to developing post- traumatic stress disorder, or PTSD, and in the 1990s this led to attempts to get people to participate in a single counselling session or psychological debriefing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This approach has not been shown to be effective. One comprehensive review on the subject concluded that compulsory counselling after an incident &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/12076399\"&gt;should be discontinued&lt;/a&gt;, and some have gone as far as to argue that a &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://bjp.rcpsych.org/content/183/1/12\"&gt;disaster industry&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo; has promoted such interventions uncritically. When you look at the evidence, it is not true that most people develop PTSD. And this isn&rsquo;t the only myth about the condition.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Immediately after a traumatic event people understandably feel shocked, anxious and afraid. Events loom large in their minds, and they might find themselves plagued for some days or weeks by flashbacks, nightmares and a constant anxiety that they are not safe. None of this is pleasant, but these are all very normal reactions to any event whether your own life threatened or you saw other people injured or dying in front of you.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02d5sf4\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Flashbacks can become so intrusive that thinking is disrupted. Feelings of guilt might lead a person to go over and over events in their mind, for instance, questioning whether they could have done more to save other people. They might go to great lengths to avoid any places or activity that remind them of the event or become numb to everything in an attempt not to feel anything at all. A constant sense of high alert can mean that someone simply walking past them in the street can set their heart racing and bring back feelings of terror.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Remarkable resilience&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href=\"http://www.nhs.uk/Conditions/Post-traumatic-stress-disorder/Pages/Symptoms.aspx\"&gt;symptoms of PTSD&lt;/a&gt; are very real and very distressing. It&rsquo;s important that anyone who experiences them can get the help they need. But one of the keys to a diagnosis of PTSD is that these symptoms persist for &lt;a href=\"http://www.dsm5.org/Documents/PTSD%20Fact%20Sheet.pdf\"&gt;at least a month after the event&lt;/a&gt;, and that they affect a person&rsquo;s work or social life, or stop them getting on with the things they want to do.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The remarkable thing is that for most people these symptoms gradually subside, and as a result PTSD is not as common as you might expect. Even in extreme situations like war most people are not affected. Only &lt;a href=\"http://www.kcl.ac.uk/kcmhr/publications/15YearReportfinal.pdf\"&gt;4% of British soldiers developed PTSD&lt;/a&gt; after serving in Iraq, for example, rising to 6.9% for those in combat roles. Among reservists rates were a little higher, averaging at 6%. Researchers think this might be because they return to their old civilian lives where they have to cope on their own, while regular members of the armed forces remain with colleagues who have a better understanding of what war is like.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02d5sqx\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Likewise after attacks on civilians, most people are not affected. A study of more than 10,000 people including police and construction workers, who came to aid of staff at World Trade Center on 11 September 2001, found that three-quarters &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/23551932\"&gt;did not have long-lasting difficulties&lt;/a&gt;. It is true that sometimes symptoms can be delayed. Some 8.5% of the responders at the World Trade Center experienced difficulties much later on. There were even cases of soldiers who fought in World War II, returned home seemingly fine, worked for the rest of their careers, and then on retirement found that the nightmares and flashbacks suddenly returned.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Norwegian experience&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p019smbv\"&gt;travelled to Norway&lt;/a&gt; to meet survivors of the shootings on Utoya Island in 2011, where 69 teenagers died and many others witnessed the killings. Some survived by hiding in the toilets for three hours, knowing that the killer was still nearby, hunting for them.&nbsp; Immediately after the event, Atle Dyregrov, Director of the Center for Crisis Psychology in Bergen estimated that between 30-50% would suffer long-term mental health difficulties including PTSD. Even though the numbers have ended up at the high end of the estimate, that still leaves half who witnessed their friends being murdered and fled for their lives, yet have somehow recovered.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02d5sc3\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The shootings took place in the summer holidays and most of the teenagers involved were starting at new schools in the autumn, which made it harder for them. But the country already had a plan for how to deal with something like this, whether a natural disaster or a crime, and provided a ground-breaking level of support. Every school involved was given advice on what to do, the strict attendance rules were relaxed for survivors and students were given the choice about when and how to talk to their new classmates about what had happened. Some even decided to avoid questions by standing up and telling the whole class about it when they were ready. The bereaved and the survivors were given an opportunity to revisit the island at different times, since it was learned from previous disasters that they come at it from such different perspectives. This kind of support did seem to make a difference and other countries are now looking to the Norwegian experience.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Exposure levels&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another myth about PTSD is that only people who are weak will experience the condition. Why, even after the most horrendous experiences, some people develop PTSD and others don&rsquo;t, is still a mystery. The psychiatrists&rsquo; bible in the US, DSM until recently included a person&rsquo;s responses to the initial event. Intense fear, helplessness or horror were thought to result in more PTSD, but those indicators have been removed from the newest version of the manual, because it&rsquo;s been found that they don&rsquo;t predict who will and won&rsquo;t suffer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The nature of the event itself can of course make a difference. Rates of PTSD amongst US troops are &lt;a href=\"http://www.nejm.org/doi/full/10.1056/NEJMoa040603\"&gt;almost triple those in the UK&lt;/a&gt;, partly because there is a higher proportion of reservists, but also because their average tour of duty is a year, compared with six months for UK service people. A review of the many different studies following up &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3386850/\"&gt;staff at World Trade Center&lt;/a&gt; found that those who were injured, worked on the higher floors, witnessed others falling or jumping from the buildings or whose employers were killed, were more likely to develop PTSD. But so were women, younger people, the less well-paid and those who had experienced other setbacks in the preceding years. So it&rsquo;s a complex picture.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02d5t7l\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It is also a myth that when children go through something traumatic they just bounce back. They too can suffer from PTSD. A review of dozens of studies of &lt;a href=\"http://www.thelancet.com/journals/lancet/article/PIIS0140-6736(11)60051-2/abstract\"&gt;displaced children&lt;/a&gt; has tried to pin down the main factors leading to PTSD. Not surprisingly exposure to violence makes a difference, but it&rsquo;s still hard to guess who will be affected, when even the number of traumatic events they&rsquo;ve experienced doesn&rsquo;t predict whether they will develop PTSD or not.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As well as the factors putting people at risk, there are also those which contribute to resilience and protect against developing PTSD. &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/23551932\"&gt;Supportive family and colleagues&lt;/a&gt;&nbsp;can definitely make a difference. But again these factors still don&rsquo;t tell us exactly who will and won&rsquo;t be affected. Until we know more about the condition, the key is to make psychological help available if and when people do need it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our&nbsp;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;&nbsp;or&nbsp;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;&nbsp;page, or message us on&nbsp;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Disclaimer&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;All content within this column is provided for general information only, and should not be treated as a substitute for the medical advice of your own doctor or any other health care professional. Always consult your own GP if you're in any way concerned about your health.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-12-01T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"PTSD: Do most people get it after terrifying incidents?","HeadlineShort":"Myths about traumatic stress","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Why the assumption that everyone is prone to developing the condition after witnessing a traumatic event isnt the only myth that exists about it.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Why the assumption that everyone is prone to developing PTSD after witnessing a traumatic event isnt the only myth that exists about it.","SummaryShort":"Why some people can cope better","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-12-01T10:53:23Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"a21afb41-d53f-4a2e-9f50-1baf7a3a838a","Id":"wwfuture/story/20141201-the-myths-about-ptsd","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-12-01T10:53:23Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20141201-the-myths-about-ptsd"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20141201-the-myths-about-ptsd","_id":"559a56470b808738c4e2fa2e"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s easy to spot the girls&rsquo; section of a children&rsquo;s clothes shop because most of it is pink. I know a lot of parents who insist that they would like their daughters to wear something different, but pink seems to hold an irresistible allure for them. But is that really true? Is it inevitable that girls are born to grow up to prefer pink? &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Various studies have looked at colour preferences in different age groups. In the US most have found that babies and toddlers, whether male or female, are attracted to primary colours such as red and blue. Pink doesn&rsquo;t feature high on the list, although it is more popular than brown and grey. &nbsp;Some studies of this age group have found blue is favoured, others red, but they rarely find any gender difference.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02c5b19\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2007, research conducted at Newcastle University in the UK asked &lt;a href=\"http://www.cell.com/current-biology/abstract/S0960-9822(07)01559-X\"&gt;adults for their favourite colour&lt;/a&gt;. Did most of the women choose pink, or even red? No. The colour which came out top, for both men and women, was blue. But women, on average, rated the reddish shades more highly than the men did. The authors speculated that this was because hunter-gatherer women traditionally had the job of collecting fruit, so they might be more attuned to reddish shades of berries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s not quite clear why this should influence their likes and dislikes. Perhaps it might lead to improved skills in discrimination between different shades of red, but there&rsquo;s a missing step in the logic. Since some red berries are delicious and others are poisonous, why should that make red your favourite colour? If women evolved to prefer red, this should be universal, but a study conducted last year with the Himba people in Namibia found there was &lt;a href=\"http://psycnet.apa.org/psycinfo/2012-30151-001/\"&gt;no preference for reddish tones&lt;/a&gt; among women. &lt;br /&gt; &lt;strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Hard-wired colours?&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Cultural norms may also shape colour preferences. In cultures where pink is considered the appropriate colour for a baby girl and blue for a baby boy, babies become accustomed from birth to spending time wearing or even surrounded by, those colours. This makes it hard to know whether any preferences expressed later on are hard-wired. But a study from 2011 tried to get closer to discovering what&rsquo;s going on.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When one-year-old girls and boys were shown pairs of identical objects such as bracelets, pill boxes and picture frames, but with one object pink and another of a second colour, they were &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.2044-835X.2011.02027.x/abstract\"&gt;no more likely to choose pink than any other colour&lt;/a&gt;. But after the age of two the girls started to like pink and, by four, boys were determined in their rejection of pink. This is the precise time when toddlers start to become aware of their gender, to talk about it and even to look around them to see what defines boy and what defines a girl. But just like adults, even very small children show biases towards their own group.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This group bias was also seen another study where three-to-five-year-olds were given &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16942493\"&gt;red or blue t-shirts to wear at nursery&lt;/a&gt;. For one group, the red and blue t-shirts were constantly referred to, and by the end of three weeks the children liked everything about their own colour group better. And that was just three weeks. Gender becomes a key topic of discussion from early pregnancy onwards. When we hear the news of the birth of a new baby, there&rsquo;s just one thing we want to know. Is it a boy or a girl?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02c5b38\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You could argue that it doesn&rsquo;t really matter what colour babies are exposed to the most, but it can even affect the way we, as adults, treat them. There&rsquo;s one famous study showing that women treated the exact same babies differently &lt;a href=\"http://www.jstor.org/discover/10.2307/1128775?uid=3738032&amp;uid=2129&amp;uid=2&amp;uid=70&amp;uid=4&amp;sid=21104447047971\"&gt;depending on whether they were dressed in pink or blue&lt;/a&gt;. If the clothes were blue they assumed it was a boy, played more physical games with them and encouraged them to play with a squeaky hammer, whereas they would gently soothe the baby dressed in pink and choose a doll for them to play with.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Pink for boys?&lt;/strong&gt; &lt;span&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/span&gt;But what about the idea that a century ago little boys were dressed in pink and pink for girls is only a recent fashion? It seems even that might be something of a myth too. Psychology writer Christian Jarrett describes in his new book Great Myths of the Brain, how an Italian psychologist Marco Del Giudice, who &lt;a href=\"http://www.researchgate.net/publication/229437155_The_Twentieth_Century_Reversal_of_Pink-Blue_Gender_Coding_A_Scientific_Urban_Legend\"&gt;tried to find the origins of this idea&lt;/a&gt;, could find just four short magazine quotes, describing pink as the colour for boys. In two of these he believes that perhaps the blue and pink were accidentally swapped around. That seems unlikely to me, but when he searched a database of five million books printed in American or British English from 1800-2000 more convincing was the lack of any mentions of &ldquo;pink for a boy&rdquo;, even though from 1890 onwards there were increasing mentions of &ldquo;pink for a girl&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even the association of pink with femininity today can backfire if it&rsquo;s not used in the right way. Pink is often used for breast cancer campaigns, but researchers at Erasmus University Rotterdam found that when &lt;a href=\"http://journals.ama.org/doi/abs/10.1509/jmkr.48.3.413\"&gt;women were shown adverts dominated by the colour pink&lt;/a&gt;, they were in fact less likely to think they&rsquo;d contract breast cancer themselves or to donate money to a cancer charity. The authors don&rsquo;t believe this was because they hated the colour pink, but because when they were reminded of their gender so overtly, the adverts felt so personally threatening that it set off denial mechanisms. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But there is one way at least in which pink can be useful for both women and men. Back in 2002 researchers in Switzerland who were keen &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/12026752\"&gt;to increase the response rate to surveys&lt;/a&gt;, found that printing questionnaires on coloured paper made no difference, unless the paper was pink, in which case 12% more people filled it in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Colours, it seems, influence our behaviour much more than we realise.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;You can hear more Medical Myths on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p002vsyw\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Health Check on the BBC World Service&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Disclaimer&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;All content within this column is provided for general information only, and should not be treated as a substitute for the medical advice of your own doctor or any other health care professional. Always consult your own GP if you're in any way concerned about your health.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The pink vs blue gender myth ","HeadlineShort":"The pink vs blue gender myth ","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Is there truth to the idea that men prefer blue and women like pink? Claudia Hammond investigates, and discovers why these colours matter more than we think.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Is there truth to the idea that men prefer blue and women like pink? Claudia Hammond investigates, and discovers why these colours matter more than we think.","SummaryShort":"How colour stereotypes change how we think","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"b8ed0fac-1f33-40c1-ab34-de5feb8f1f6e","Id":"wwfuture/story/20141117-the-pink-vs-blue-gender-myth","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20141117-the-pink-vs-blue-gender-myth"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20141117-the-pink-vs-blue-gender-myth","_id":"559a56450b808738c4e2fa0c"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If you find yourself about to go down with a cold this winter, the chances are that at some point a friend will suggest you take echinacea. Some swear by it to ward off a cold when they feel the first stirrings of a sore throat. Others take it once a cold is full-blown, in the hope that it will speed their recovery. Native Americans have long valued &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/12808356\"&gt;echinacea for its medical properties&lt;/a&gt;, but in the 20th Century its use spread to many countries after it became popular in Germany. In today&rsquo;s health food shops &shy; you can see dozens of different kinds of preparations. The question is, does it work?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Every couple of years a new study is published showing that echinacea either does or doesn&rsquo;t reduce your risk of contracting a cold. Part of the reason for this mixed picture is that it comes in so many forms. Of the nine different species, all from the daisy family, there are three which are often used medicinally - the pinky-purple &lt;em&gt;echinacea purpurea&lt;/em&gt;, the pale purple coneflower and the slightly shorter &lt;em&gt;echinacea angustifolia&lt;/em&gt;. To complicate things more, some preparations use the root, others the flower, the leaves or the whole plant and then it can be pressed for its juice, made into a tincture or dried and put into tablets. Different research studies use different preparations, making them hard to compare.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02b11cj\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;No one is even agreed on exactly which ingredients in echinacea might prevent or aid recovery from a cold, or whether it&rsquo;s the combination of ingredients that&rsquo;s crucial. Echinacea contains four types of compounds which might boost the immune system: &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17538868\"&gt;alkamides&lt;/a&gt;, glycoproteins, polysaccharides and caffeic acid derivatives, but not all these substances are found in every species of echinacea, nor in every part of the plant.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So does it make a difference to your chances of contracting a cold? After years of mixed results, in 2007 &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17597571\"&gt;scientists at the University Of Connecticut in the US conducted a meta-analysis&lt;/a&gt;, combining and reanalysing the data from the 1600 participants in previous trials. The results seemed good news for echinacea fans, with newspapers proclaiming that supplements could halve your chances of getting a cold. The problem is that the plant is so versatile that the original studies involved not only different species of echinacea, but different parts of the plant, extracted in different ways. You could argue that this is like pooling the results of studies measuring different treatments, since chemically not every species of part of the plant is the same.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Full review&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then earlier this year came the most comprehensive review so far &ndash; &lt;a href=\"http://summaries.cochrane.org/CD000530/ARI_echinacea-for-preventing-and-treating-the-common-cold\"&gt;a Cochrane review that scanned the literature and included only the very best studies&lt;/a&gt;. They looked for randomised controlled studies where people were given either echinacea or a placebo and neither they nor the staff administering the preparations knew which they were getting. From the 82 trials they assessed they ended up with 24 which fulfilled the criteria, mostly from the US and Germany. Still, they weren&rsquo;t always convinced that the people wouldn&rsquo;t guess what they were taking. One trial used capsules containing vegetable oil as a placebo. Another used the manufacturers&rsquo; own staff who might be familiar with the taste of their own product.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02b129q\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In some studies people were given echinacea and then deliberately exposed to a cold virus to see whether they became infected. In others, people had the echinacea on hand and were instructed to start taking it the moment they felt a tickle in their throat or starting sneezing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Their results were slightly disappointing for those hoping to avoid colds in the future. They found that when you looked at these well-conducted trials, none of them showed on their own that echinacea prevented colds. But on a more positive note, when they pooled the results of the best studies, giving them a much larger group of people, those who took echinacea did turn out to be less likely to get a cold, even if only 10 to 20% less likely. The authors suggest that perhaps the reason that these effects didn&rsquo;t show up in individual studies, is that they had such small numbers of people taking part in them. Of course with the pooled result there&rsquo;s still the problem that the people in different studies took different forms of echinacea. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Immune trigger&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We also need to bear in mind that these studies all excluded people with an underlying illness. Not everyone wants to boost their immune systems. A healthy immune system has been described to me as a dog on a lead that&rsquo;s out in the park. It&rsquo;s straining at the leash ready to shoot off at any moment if it can get free. You want it to keep on walking at a steady pace with you, not to break free and go rushing off too far. Similarly, you want the immune system to work reliably, but not to go too far. Auto-immune diseases like multiple sclerosis and rheumatoid arthritis involve an immune system that has become too active and organisations such as the National Institute of Health in the US &lt;a href=\"http://www.nlm.nih.gov/medlineplus/druginfo/natural/981.html\"&gt;advise patients with these conditions not to take echinacea&lt;/a&gt;.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So the next time you feel your throat getting a bit sore and fear you&rsquo;re going down with a cold, you could try taking some echinacea, but bear in mind no one yet knows exactly which kind you should take. And until they do, then on average there&rsquo;s an 80-90% chance you will still get the cold. It might make a difference, but only a small one.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Disclaimer&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;All content within this column is provided for general information only, and should not be treated as a substitute for the medical advice of your own doctor or any other health care professional. is the drug&rdquo; sang Roxy Music back in the early 1970s, and it turns out Bryan Ferry and team were on to something.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When you fall in love, the same areas of your brain affected by alcohol or drugs like cocaine are activated. They stimulate the part of our brain that seeks rewards. And we become, in a way, addicted to those feelings.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\" http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140331-can-you-die-of-a-broken-heart\"&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01wj6rx\"}}&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140331-can-you-die-of-a-broken-heart\"&gt;Read more: Can you die of a broken heart&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So when we fall out of love &ndash; or when we break up with someone we&rsquo;re still in love with &ndash; the effects can be as drastic as suddenly giving up a substance we&rsquo;re addicted to. The Brit Lab, just in time for Valentine&rsquo;s Day, investigated these very real effects.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Share this story on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/bbc_future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262db"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dc"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2015-02-13T10:51:52Z","HeadlineLong":"How love and heartache affect the body","HeadlineShort":"How heartbreak affects the body","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Falling in love  and suffering a broken heart  have some powerful effects on the body. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Can you be &lt;a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NHHEcmZtJvY\"&gt;too beautiful&lt;/a&gt;? It is hardly a problem that most of us have to contemplate &ndash; as much as we might like to dream that it were the case.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;When treating people for pain, doctors tend to take less care over the more attractive people&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet the blessings and curses of beauty have been a long-standing interest in psychology. Do those blessed with symmetrical features and a striking figure live in a cloud of appreciation &ndash; or does it sometimes pay to be plain?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Combing through decades of findings, social psychologists Lisa Slattery Walker and Tonya Frevert at the University of North Carolina at Charlotte have &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/soc4.12132/abstract\"&gt;reviewed all the evidence to date&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; and their conclusions are not what you might expect.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At the most superficial level, beauty might be thought to carry a kind of halo around it; we see that someone has one good attribute, and by association, our subconscious assumes that they have been blessed in other departments too. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s one of many status characteristics that we can identify very early in our interactions,&rdquo; says Walker.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02jxh4y\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To psychologists, this is called the &ldquo;what is beautiful is good&rdquo; heuristic, but fans of the sitcom 30 Rock might recognise this as &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Bubble_%2830_Rock%29\"&gt;the bubble&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo;. Jon Hamm&rsquo;s character is remarkably incompetent, yet manages to live in blissful self-delusion thanks to his good looks. As a doctor, for example, he can&rsquo;t even perform the Heimlich manoeuvre, but somehow managed to drift through medical school thanks to his natural charm.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to the available evidence, the bubble is a reality. In education, for instance, Walker and Frevert found a wealth of research showing that better looking students, at school and university, tend to be judged by teachers as being more competent and intelligent &ndash; and that was reflected in the grades they gave them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What&rsquo;s more, the bubble&rsquo;s influence inflates over the years. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a cumulative effect,&rdquo; explains Frevert. &ldquo;You become more confident and have more positive beliefs and more opportunities to demonstrate your competence.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the workplace, your face really can be your fortune. When everything else is considered, more attractive people tend to earn more money and climb higher on the corporate ladder than people who are considered less pleasing on the eye. One study of MBA graduates found that there was about a 10 to 15% difference in earnings between the most and least attractive people in the group &ndash; which &lt;a href=\"http://digitalcommons.ilr.cornell.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1045&amp;context=chrr\"&gt;added up to about $230,000 (&pound;150,000) over a lifetime&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;You are being conferred advantages throughout your life, from your schooldays into the workplace,&rdquo; says Walker.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02jxh19\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even in the courts, a pleasing appearance can work its magic. Attractive defendants are likely to get more lenient sentences, or to escape conviction entirely; attractive plaintiffs, meanwhile, are more likely to win their case and get bigger financial settlements. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a pervasive effect,&rdquo; says Walker.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But if beauty pays in most circumstances, there are still situations where it can backfire. While attractive men may be considered better leaders, for instance, implicit sexist prejudices can work against attractive women, &lt;a href=\"http://psycnet.apa.org/journals/zfp/220/2/98/\"&gt;making them less likely to be hired for high-level jobs&lt;/a&gt; that require authority. (If you want Hollywood&rsquo;s take on this truism, Frevert and Walker suggest that you look no further than Reese Witherspoon&rsquo;s Legally Blonde.) And as you might expect, good-looking people of both genders run into jealousy &ndash; one study found that if you are interviewed by someone of the same sex, they may be less likely to &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S002210311000123X\"&gt;recruit you&lt;/a&gt; if they judge that you are more attractive than they are.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02jxh7g\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;More worryingly, being beautiful or handsome could harm your medical care. We tend to link good looks to health, meaning that illnesses are often taken less seriously when they affect the good-looking. When treating people for pain, for instance, doctors tend to take less care over the more attractive people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And the bubble of beauty can be a somewhat lonely place. One study in 1975, for instance, found that people tend to move further away from a beautiful woman on the pathway &ndash; perhaps as a mark of respect, but still making interaction more distant. &ldquo;Attractiveness can convey more power over visible space &ndash; but that in turn can make others feel they can&rsquo;t approach that person,&rdquo; says Frevert. Interestingly, the online dating website OKCupid recently reported that people with the most flawlessly beautiful profile pictures are less likely to find dates than those with &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/news/magazine-31168242\"&gt;quirkier, less perfect pics&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; perhaps because the prospective dates are less intimidated.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02jxgwy\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So, as you might have guessed, being beautiful is not a passport to certain happiness &ndash; though it helps. Frevert and Walker are keen to emphasise that like our conceptions of beauty itself, these influences are superficial and by no means deep-rooted in our biology, as some might suggest. &ldquo;We have a whole set of cultural ideals about beauty that let us say if someone is attractive &ndash; and through those same ideals, we begin to associate it with competence,&rdquo; says Walker. In a sense, it&rsquo;s just a cognitive shortcut for a quick appraisal. &ldquo;And like many of the shortcuts we use, it&rsquo;s not very reliable,&rdquo; says Frevert. And it could be fairly easy to lessen the impact &ndash; if human resources departments give more information about a candidate&rsquo;s achievements before an interview, for example.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ultimately, Frevert points out that focusing too much on your appearance can itself be detrimental if it creates undue stress and anxiety &ndash; even for those already blessed with good looks. &ldquo;If you are obsessing about attractiveness, it may alter your experience and interactions,&rdquo; she says. It&rsquo;s a cliche, but no amount of beauty can make up for a bad personality. As the writer Dorothy Parker put it so elegantly: &ldquo;Beauty is only skin deep, but ugly goes clean to the bone.&rdquo;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Share this story on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/bbc_future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2015-02-13T10:25:32Z","HeadlineLong":"The surprising downsides of being drop dead gorgeous","HeadlineShort":"The downsides of being beautiful","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Good looks can get you far in life, but psychologists say there are unrecognised pitfalls for the beautiful.
