teTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p02gsk90","_id":"559a553e0b808738c4e2d4f5"}],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":55720,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/2g/sk/p02gskkn.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"Babies have an instinct to suck anything that brushes their lips (Thinkstock)","SynopsisMedium":"Babies have an instinct to suck anything that brushes their lips (Thinkstock)","SynopsisShort":"(Thinkstock)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/2g/sk/p02gskkn.jpg","Title":"178052393-Thinkstock-baby-sucking-thumb.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p02gskkn","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p02gskkn","_id":"559a553e0b808738c4e2d4f6"},{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":114538,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/2g/sk/p02gsktz.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"(Thinkstock)","SynopsisMedium":"(Thinkstock)","SynopsisShort":"(Thinkstock)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/2g/sk/p02gsktz.jpg","Title":"146737543-Monkeys-kissing.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p02gsktz","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p02gsktz","_id":"559a553e0b808738c4e2d4f8"},{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":94093,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/2g/sk/p02gskq7.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"(Thinkstock)","SynopsisMedium":"(Thinkstock)","SynopsisShort":"(Thinkstock)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/2g/sk/p02gskq7.jpg","Title":"470447709-Thinkstock-kissing.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p02gskq7","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p02gskq7","_id":"559a553e0b808738c4e2d4f7"}],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Jason G Goldman is a\nscience writer based in Los Angeles. He writes about animal behavior, wildlife,\nand the complex relationship between humans and the rest of the animal kingdom.\nFind him on twitter: &lt;a href=\"http://twitter.com/jgold85\"&gt;@jgold85&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Jason G Goldman","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:17Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"09cd12f4-0173-4bae-9a62-2613a216e20c","Id":"wwfuture/author/jason-goldman","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:17Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"jason-goldman"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/jason-goldman","_id":"559a524d0b808738c4e25f79"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;They kind of flap around on the front of your face, get dried out and chapped in the winter, and occasionally get caught up between your teeth and mistaken for food. Seriously, what good are lips? Birds get on just fine without them, turtles' lips have hardened into beaks as well, and while most mammals have lips, we humans are in a class all our own having lips that are permanently turned outwards.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It turns out that lips are quite important; so important, even, that it seems worth the risk of having your bottom lip caught in between your chompers. Even though that can &lt;em&gt;really&lt;/em&gt; hurt.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Using our lips to suck is one of the very first skills we have after we're born. In fact, it's so fundamental to our survival that it's known as a \"primitive reflex\"; we're born knowing how to suck and no learning is necessary. That's true for nearly all mammals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02gskkn\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It's the sucking reflex combined with another primitive response, the rooting reflex, that allows infants to breastfeed. The rooting reflex works by turning the infant's head to face anything that strokes its mouth or cheek. As soon as something grazes the newborn's lips, the sucking reflex is activated. While the tongue then does a lot of the work, the lips are vital to maintain a tight seal so that the infant can swallow.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That means feeding, whether from breast or from bottle, is not a passive behaviour on the part of a newborn infant. It's more like a conversation, with each side doing his or her part in a dance elaborately choreographed by evolution. At the centre of that dance are the lips.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Read my lips&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;The lips are of course also important in the act of eating other foods, and in speech. In linguistics, the lips are two of many &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Place_of_articulation\"&gt;places of articulation&lt;/a&gt;, or spots in the mouth and throat that aid in blockage of air moving out from the lungs. Bring your two lips together and you can form the sounds &lt;em&gt;p&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;b&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;m&lt;/em&gt;. To make the sounds &lt;em&gt;f&lt;/em&gt; or &lt;em&gt;v&lt;/em&gt;, bring your lower lip to your upper teeth. To make a &lt;em&gt;w&lt;/em&gt; sound, move the back of your tongue towards the roof of your mouth while also moving your lips closer together.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Speech is arguably a critical part of human life, but it's not quite as much fun as kissing. Kissing isn't universal, though it does pop up in some 90% of cultures. Darwin himself noted that there are cultures in which kissing is conspicuously absent. \"We Europeans are so accustomed to kissing as a mark of affection that it might be thought to be innate in mankind,\" he wrote in &lt;a href=\"https://books.google.com/books?id=KGNHAAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA216&amp;lpg=PA216&amp;dq=%22is+replaced+in+various+parts+of+the+world+by+the+rubbing+of+noses%22&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=wbZSh8FASS&amp;sig=MH0c-sfb4iD4a-rheldDVqQZvzE&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=HcmQVP35ApK5ogTJ5ILwDg&amp;ved=0CCgQ6AEwAg#v=onepage&amp;q=%22is%20replaced%20in%20various%20parts%20of%20the%20world%20by%20the%20rubbing%20of%20noses%22&amp;f=false\"&gt;The Expression of Emotion in Man and Animals&lt;/a&gt;, \"but this is not the case&hellip;[it is] unknown with the [Maori] New Zealanders, Tahitians, Papuans, Australians, Somals of Africa, and the Esquimaux [Eskimo].\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02gsktz\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If kissing isn't universal, it could still have its roots in biology, perhaps as a combination of inherited impulses and learned behaviour. For one thing, other species kiss as well. Chimpanzees do it in order to reconcile following a fight, and bonobos do it with some tongue as well.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In a 2008 issue of &lt;a href=\"http://www.scientificamerican.com/article/affairs-of-the-lips-why-we-kiss/\"&gt;Scientific American Mind&lt;/a&gt; the writer Chip Walter argued, citing British zoologist Desmond Morris, that kissing may have originated from the primate behavior of pre-chewing food and passing it to the kids. Chimpanzee mothers, for example, are known to chew food and before swallowing they press their lips to the lips of their youngsters to allow the food to pass into their mouths. The pressing of lips may have then become a general means for relieving anxiety. Basic classical conditioning would suggest that by pairing lip stimulation with food, simply touching the lips would eventually trigger feelings of pleasure. Add to that the overabundance of nerve endings in the lips, and you have the recipe for ecstasy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Lip service&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;Lips are exquisitely sensitive (if sometimes slimy) bits of tissue. The part of the brain responsible for detecting touch is called the somatosensory cortex, and is found on the top of the brain in an area called the postcentral gyrus. Touch sensations from all over the body get sent there to be processed, with each part of the body getting its own little sub-division along the postcentral gyrus, and the size reflects the density of receptors rather than the amount of skin available to touch. For example, the part devoted to sensations from the chest and stomach is fairly small. Meanwhile, the parts that process sensations from the &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20141215-why-are-most-of-us-right-handed\"&gt;hands&lt;/a&gt; and lips are enormous. Just as the hands are a central conduit with which we experience the world, so too are the lips.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02gskq7\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to researcher &lt;a href=\"http://psycnet.apa.org/psycinfo/2008-06860-010\"&gt;Gordon Gallup&lt;/a&gt;, in cultures without kissing, \"sex partners may blow in each other&rsquo;s faces, lick, suck, or rub their partner&rsquo;s face prior to intercourse.\" But the so-called \"Eskimo kiss\" isn't actually about rubbing noses, as early Arctic explorers thought, but about sniffing &ndash; &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20131104-natures-grossest-perfume\"&gt;exchanging scents&lt;/a&gt;. It's possible that the act of kissing could have come along as an enjoyable means for sniffing out possible romantic partners.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Gallup studied kissing behavior in a group who should know a thing or two about it: American college students. He and his colleagues found that one of the chief means through which college-aged women determined whether a partner was a good kisser was based upon chemical cues, taste and smell. And according to his research, college women said they were far less likely to have &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20130221-the-wilder-side-of-sex\"&gt;sex&lt;/a&gt; with a man unless they'd kissed him first. Whether humans indeed have pheromones, and whether we can even detect them if we do, kissing facilitates the sharing of body odours, which can relay a wealth of information regarding things like basic hygiene. In another survey conducted by Gallup, he asked \"Have you ever found yourself attracted to someone, only to discover after kissing them for the first time that you were no longer interested?\" Of the men he surveyed, 59% said yes, while 66% of the women agreed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While his studies are quite obviously limited by focusing on American college students, when placed alongside the cross-cultural data and evidence from animal research, it seems reasonable to conclude that the close contact that kissing offers facilitates a good deal of sniffing. Whether we're aware of it or not, kissing may allow us to judge the potential suitability of those we desire.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And that&rsquo;s why the occasional chapped lip, or mistaken bite of this hypersensitive part of the body, is all worth it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Share this story on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/bbc_future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"The mysteries of our bodies. Jason G Goldman investigates why our body parts  from eyes to hands  ended up the way they did, and how they made us human.","Name":"Body Matters","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Body Matters","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fa461851-f43f-466f-9051-c20a0acdc6cc","Id":"wwfuture/column/body-matters","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/body-matters"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fa461851-f43f-466f-9051-c20a0acdc6cc","Id":"wwfuture/column/body-matters","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/body-matters"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/body-matters","_id":"559a52610b808738c4e26299"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262db"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dd"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2015-01-14T09:27:01Z","HeadlineLong":"Evolution: Why do we have lips?","HeadlineShort":"Why do we have lips?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Human beings are unusual in that our mouths are surrounded by hypersensitive, easily-hurt pink tissue  why? Jason G Goldman investigates.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Take a stroll through a zoo and, if you're paying attention, you might notice that most animals can be sorted into two groups. There are those animals with eyes on the sides of their heads &ndash; chickens, cows, horses, zebras &ndash; and then there are those with eyes that are closer together on the front of their face, like monkeys, tigers, owls, wolves. All the humans visiting the zoo are obviously in the latter camp. What's behind this divide?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There's a trade-off when it comes to eye placement. As the eyes move forward along the face, two fields of vision overlap. It's that overlap &ndash; the slightly different perspective on the scene in front of you that each of your two eyes sends to your brain &ndash; that allows you to perceive depth. Animals with sideways-facing eyes may not have this well-developed depth perception, but they are able to see an extremely wide panorama instead.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p028kyl3\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Eye placement probably evolved for different reasons in different groups of animals. Some turtles, for example, have &lt;a href=\"http://blogs.scientificamerican.com/thoughtful-animal/2014/03/19/the-turtle-with-human-eyes/\"&gt;eyes on the side of their heads but process optical information as if their eyes were forward facing&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; probably because when they retract their heads into their carapaces their eyes only receive light from the front, as if their eyes faced forwards. But how did our corner of the family tree, the primates, wind up with forward-facing eyes? There are lots of ideas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 1922, Edward Treacher Collins, a British ophthalmologist, wrote that early primates needed sight that would \"enable them to swing and spring with accuracy from bough to bough&hellip;to grasp food with their hands and convey it by them to their mouths\". As our primate ancestors moved into the trees to escape their predators, the needs to navigate the tree branches and to grab rapidly fleeing prey with their hands, he argued, meant that evolution favoured a visual system with good depth perception.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p028kz9b\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Collins' idea has become known as the \"arboreal locomotion hypothesis\" &ndash; arboreal meaning living in trees. In the decades since, it has been expanded and refined, but the basic idea that our ancestors evolved forward facing eyes to accurately judge distances while leaping from tree to tree remained central for quite a while. After all, the stakes for failing to work out the true distance between trees were high. &ldquo;The price of failure was to drop many metres onto a ground inhabited by carnivorous beasts,\" wrote visual psychotherapist Christopher Tyler in 1991.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The problem with Collins&rsquo; hypothesis is that many animals that thrive in trees have eyes on the sides of their heads &ndash; squirrels, for instance. So, in 2005, biological anthropologist &lt;a href=\"http://www.bu.edu/anthrop/people/faculty/m-cartmill/\"&gt;Matt Cartmill&lt;/a&gt; proposed a different idea: &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/evan.1360010308/abstract\"&gt;the \"visual predation hypothesis\".&lt;/a&gt; Predators are best served, ostensibly, by having extremely good depth perception. That would help them to better locate and more effectively take down their prey, whether that's a leopard stalking a gazelle or a raptor snatching a rabbit in its talons, or one of our primate ancestors grabbing an insect from the branch of a tree. Cartmill thought his explanation was the most elegant, because it also explained other evolutionary changes that are distinctive to primates. Early primates, for example, hunt by sight rather than by scent. Cartmill thought that the reduction in their ability to smell was a side effect of the eyes' convergence, simply because the space available for the nose and its connections to the brain became smaller as it was crowded out by the eyes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p028kznq\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Neurobiologist John Allman picked up on Cartmill's hypothesis and expanded it to focus on nocturnal predation. Not all predators, after all, have forward facing eyes. Cats, primates and owls do, but not mongooses, tree shrews, and robins. Allman's contribution was to suggest that forward-facing eyes proved beneficial for creatures that hunt at night, such as owls and cats, because they can take in more light than sideways-facing eyes. As it happens, early primates were also nocturnal hunters, and their adaptation for nighttime predation may have granted forward-facing eyes to all their descendants, including our own species.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Theoretical neurobiologist &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140926-are-hairy-faces-less-evolved\"&gt;Mark Changizi&lt;/a&gt; has yet another idea. In 2008 in the &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0022519308003585\"&gt;Journal of Theoretical Biology&lt;/a&gt;, he offered up the \"X-ray vision hypothesis\". In short, forward-facing eyes allowed our ancestors to see through the dense leaves and branches in their forest habitat. The catchy name for his hypothesis comes from a curious phenomenon. \"When you hold up your finger vertically and fixate your eyes on something far beyond it,\" he writes, \"you perceive two copies of your finger, and both copies of your finger appear transparent.\" Thus, you have the ability to \"see through\" your finger, as though you were seeing with X-ray vision.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p028l016\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The clutter problem is unique to large-bodied animals in forests, such as primates. Smaller animals, such as squirrels, suffer less clutter because their heads are small enough to see in between branches and leaves. And large animals in non-forest environments can do just fine with eyes that face sideways.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So the reason we all have peepers on the front of our heads is by no means settled. Each hypothesis has its own strengths and weaknesses. But whether our eyes moved forward to account for leaping through branches, chasing after tasty bugs, or to see through leaves, at least one thing is certain: it all comes down to life in the trees.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt; or&nbsp;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;Google+&lt;/a&gt; page, or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-10-28T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Evolution: Why do your eyes face forwards?","HeadlineShort":"Why do your eyes face forwards?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Why dont we have peepers protruding from the sides of our heads? It's partly for 3D vision, but as Jason G Goldman discovers, that's not the only reason.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Why dont we have eyes protruding from the sides of our heads? It's partly for 3D vision, but as Jason G Goldman discovers, that's not the only reason.","SummaryShort":"Its a trickier question than it seems","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"a2de4bfa-53bb-4692-99d5-67ec7af73476","Id":"wwfuture/story/20141013-why-do-your-eyes-face-forwards","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20141013-why-do-your-eyes-face-forwards"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20141013-why-do-your-eyes-face-forwards","_id":"559a56410b808738c4e2f993"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Have you ever stopped to consider your face? Compared to most of the rest of the animal kingdom, the human face has at least one really peculiar feature: it's almost completely devoid of hair. Sure, some people grow beards or moustaches, but even a full pirate's beard would leave quite a bit of skin showing. They don't call us \"the hairless ape\" for nothing. How did we come to be so bare-faced?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The reasons we lost our body hair are still debated. Some researchers think that we lost our fur to &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2003/08/19/science/why-humans-and-their-fur-parted-ways.html\"&gt;free ourselves of parasites&lt;/a&gt;, such as lice. This might have made us more attractive to the opposite sex; bare skin would advertise our lack of parasites. Others have suggested that we lost most of our hair to &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1469-7998.2007.00295.x/abstract?\"&gt;facilitate cooling&lt;/a&gt; as we moved from the shady forests to the hot savannah. Still others wonder whether nakedness is one of a number of juvenile traits that humans retain throughout their lifetimes; humans are thought to be, in one sense, juvenilised apes, who mature slower and live longer than our ape cousins.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet Mark Changizi, a neurobiologist at 2AI Labs in Boston, has an intriguing alternative explanation for why we don&rsquo;t have hair on our faces, at least when we compare ourselves to other primates. It's because we're walking, talking, breathing &lsquo;mood rings&rsquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Colour reveal&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Mood rings were a short-lived fad of the mid 1970s. The idea behind wearing one was that it would act as a sort of emotional barometer, betraying your innermost feelings to anyone who took a glance at the jewellery. In truth, mood rings were little more than thermometers, designed to change colour according to body temperature. But the idea &ndash; that colour betrays emotion &ndash; isn't actually all that farfetched. The idea is pervasive in human culture. We become \"green with envy\". We turn red when angry or embarrassed. Sadness is referred to as \"the blues\". The truth may not be so far off.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p027dfhn\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Changizi, together with researchers Qiong Zhang and Shinsuke Shimojo, argues that our &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1618887/\"&gt;faces evolved their hairlessness to allow other members of our species the ability to read our emotions&lt;/a&gt;. Indeed, primate faces and &ndash; in some cases &ndash; rumps and genitalia change colours thanks to the underlying physiology of the skin. \"For highly social animals like most primates, one of the most important kinds of objects to be competent at perceiving and discriminating is other members of one's own species,\" he writes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most mammals, like dogs, horses, or bears, can only see blends of two colours when they look at the world. They're called dichromats, and they can only see yellows, blues, or the greens that form when blue and yellow mix &ndash; in addition to perceiving brightness. That's because their eyes have, in addition to the brightness-sensing rods, only two types of cones: ones sensitive to short or long wavelengths of light. But humans and some other primates are trichromats. We have a third type of cone, sensitive to medium wavelengths of light. Having that third cone means that we can also detect colours along a red-green continuum. (&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140905-the-women-with-super-human-vision\"&gt;A few lucky people are tetrachromats&lt;/a&gt;, which means they can see even more shades).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Blood flow&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But there's something odd about the three types of cones that trichromats have: they're not evenly spaced out. It just so happens that this odd assortment of cones allows our eyes to perceive properties of the blood circling through our bodies just beneath the skin: how saturated with oxygen the haemoglobin is, and how concentrated the haemoglobin is. Changes in those two variables result in predictable changes in skin colouration.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It's actually quite remarkable how many colours human skin can be. We're used to thinking of skin as being white, black, or brown. But those are just a baseline. Darker faces still blush, after all, something that even Darwin noticed. Skin appears redder as haemoglobin becomes more and more oxygenated. Reductions in oxygen saturation turn the skin green (which is just how veins carrying de-oxygenated blood back to the heart look). On the other hand, a greater accumulation of blood in a certain area turns the skin somewhat blue. Just like a bruise. And a reduction in blood concentration does the opposite, making the skin appear yellow. Which, indeed, is how we describe somebody who isn't looking very healthy. \"Colour is sufficiently suggestive of emotion,\" writes Changizi, \"that cartoons often use colour on a face to indicate emotional state.\" And it's something that even children can recognise effortlessly.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p027dfkh\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Dichromats &ndash; the dogs, bunnies, and bears of the world, along with certain primates &ndash; can only perceive changes along the blue-yellow dimension, allowing them to notice changes only in blood concentration. In some ways, that's a useful skill. It's probably best to avoid someone who looks yellow, to avoid becoming infected with whatever disease or sickness they're trying to fight off, or to escape being near someone who might imminently vomit. On the other hand, a bruise betrays injury, someone who will be easier to beat in a fight over resources.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here's the best part: if it's true that facial bare skin evolved to allow for colour signalling, then other trichromatic primates should have visible skin too. To see if his hypothesis held up, Changizi looked at 97 different primate species. Sure enough, he found that monochromatic and dichromatic primates are furry, while those, including us, who have three types of cones have more visible facial skin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if bare skin was initially selected for something other than colour signalling, it is likely the case that both colour vision and visible skin became evolutionarily entangled, at least among primates, argues Changizi.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So next time your friends ask you what you're feeling, just tell them to look at your skin. They should be able to figure it out: after all, they have millions of years of evolution on their side.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-09-26T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Evolution: Why dont we have hairier faces?","HeadlineShort":"Are hairy faces less evolved?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Men may have beards, but compared to many of our primate cousins our faces are pretty hairless  why is that? Jason G Goldman explores the enigma of our bare-faced cheeks.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Is a hairless face a mark of evolutionary superiority? Jason G Goldman explores the enigma of humanitys bare-faced cheeks.","SummaryShort":"The reason behind our bare-faced cheeks","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:17Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"0ab2ef9f-7af3-4bbe-b27c-81c4bc8c2e5b","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140926-are-hairy-faces-less-evolved","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:17Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140926-are-hairy-faces-less-evolved"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140926-are-hairy-faces-less-evolved","_id":"559a56400b808738c4e2f965"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;We humans don&rsquo;t typically agree on all that much, but there is at least one thing that an impressive amount of us accept: which hand is easiest to control. If you use one hand for writing, you probably use the same one for eating as well, and most of us &ndash; around 85% of our species &ndash; prefer our right hands. In fact, \"&lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0047248409001304\"&gt;there has never been any report of a human population in which left-handed individuals predominate&lt;/a&gt;\", according to archaeologist Natalie Uomini at the University of Liverpool in the UK.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lateralisation of limb use &ndash; that is, a bias towards one side or the other &ndash; usually begins in the brain. We know that some tasks are largely controlled by brain activity in the left hemisphere, while the right hemisphere governs other tasks. Confusingly, there is some crossing of nerves between the body and the brain, which means it&rsquo;s actually the left side of the brain that has more control over the right side of the body and vice versa. In other words, the brain&rsquo;s left hemisphere helps control the operation of the right hand, eye, leg and so on.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02f8rk7\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some argue that &lt;a href=\"http://www.scientificamerican.com/article/evolutionary-origins-of-your-right-and-left-brain/\"&gt;this division of neurological labour has been a feature of animals for half a billion years&lt;/a&gt;. Perhaps it evolved because it is more efficient to allow the two hemispheres to carry out different computations at the same time. The left side of the brain, for instance, might have evolved to carry out routine operations &ndash; things like foraging for food &ndash; while the right side was kept free to detect and react rapidly to unexpected challenges in the environment &ndash; an approaching predator, for instance. This can be seen in various fish, toads and birds, &lt;a href=\"http://www.newscientist.com/article/mg20627581.600-southpaws-the-evolution-of-handedness.html#.VI74thD2fEc\"&gt;which are all more likely to attack prey seen in the right eye&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So it is possible (though hard to prove) that as our hominin ancestors began walking on two legs rather than four, freeing up their hands to perform new tasks like making tools, they were predisposed to begin using those hands differently. Or, as cognitive scientist Stephanie Braccini and colleagues put it in a Journal of Human Evolution study, \"&lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0047248409002218\"&gt;a strengthening of individual asymmetry [may have] started as soon as early hominins assumed a habitual upright posture during tool use or foraging&lt;/a&gt;\".&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02f8r8j\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In support of the idea, Braccini and her colleagues&rsquo; looked at handedness in chimpanzees, and found that when the apes stand on all fours, they displayed no real hand preferences. It was only when forced to assume an upright stance that a lateral preference emerged &ndash; although individual chimps in the study were equally likely to be left-handed as right-handed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Evidently, then, something else was needed to push early humans from a lateral preference in general to the extremely high levels of right-handedness we see today.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02f8rn8\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We know roughly when that change occurred from experiments in which researchers made their own versions of ancient stone tools using either their left or right hands to chip &ndash; or knap &ndash; the tool into shape, before comparing them with the tools made by early hominins. Doing so suggests &lt;a href=\"http://pcwww.liv.ac.uk/~nuomini/Uomini_2005_Lejre-report.pdf\"&gt;there is only limited evidence that hominin toolmakers working more than 2 million years ago were primarily right-handed&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0047248485800877\"&gt;stone tools&lt;/a&gt; that were made some 1.5 million years ago in Koobi Fora, Kenya, by two ancient human species &ndash; &lt;em&gt;Homo habilis&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;Homo erectus&lt;/em&gt; &ndash; do show some evidence of species-wide right-handedness. And by the time a species called &lt;em&gt;Homo heidelbergensis&lt;/em&gt; had appeared, perhaps around 600,000 years ago, there was a &lt;a href=\"http://www.ucl.ac.uk/~tcrnjst/SteeleUomini2009.pdf\"&gt;clear right-handed preference in prehistoric societies&lt;/a&gt;. Wear on the preserved teeth of &lt;em&gt;Homo heidelbergensis&lt;/em&gt;, for instance, suggest that food was usually brought to the mouth with the right hand.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02f8rl8\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This tells us when that shift occurred, but not why. Some have argued that it all comes down to &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20121016-is-language-unique-to-humans\"&gt;language&lt;/a&gt;. Just as most people are right-handed &ndash; a trait, remember, controlled by the left side of the brain &ndash; so do most people do the bulk of their linguistic processing in their brain&rsquo;s left hemisphere. Indeed, this left-brained specialisation for language is even more common than right-handedness &ndash; which might suggest that as the left hemisphere evolved for language, the preference for the right hand may have intensified simply as a side effect. This is called the &lt;em&gt;Homo loquens&lt;/em&gt; hypothesis: lateralisation in general was driven by the evolution of an upright, bipedal stance, while the rightward preference was driven, some time later, by the evolution of language.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Right-handedness, then, may simply be an accidental by-product of the way most of our brains are wired up. But proving the hypothesis is difficult, or even impossible, since it would ideally involve running neurological tests on our long-dead ancestors. The truth is we'll probably never quite know what the sequence of events was that led our species to lean so overwhelmingly on the right sides of our bodies and the left sides of our brains.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As for the left-handers out there? Take heart! According to a 1977 paper in the journal &lt;a href=\"http://psycnet.apa.org/journals/bul/84/3/385/\"&gt;Psychological Bulletin&lt;/a&gt;, \"there is remarkably little evidence for any association of left-handedness with deficit, as has often been suggested\". In fact, some research shows that left-handed folks might even have an easier time recovering from brain damage. And their left hand seems to have the advantage of surprise in a fight, which means they can be &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/23742683\"&gt;better at combat sports&lt;/a&gt;. All of which suggests there are advantages to breaking from the norm.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/dialog/feed?app_id=58567469885&amp;redirect_uri=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bbc.com%2Fmodules%2Fshare%2Fclose&amp;link=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bbc.com%2Ffuture%2Fstory%2F20141215-why-are-most-of-us-right-handed&amp;picture=http%3A%2F%2Fichef.bbci.co.uk%2Fwwfeatures%2F976_549%2Fimages%2Flive%2Fp0%2F2f%2F8r%2Fp02f8rdj.jpg&amp;name=&amp;description=&amp;caption=&amp;display=popup&amp;\"&gt;Share this story on Facebook&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-12-16T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Evolution: Why are most of us right-handed?","HeadlineShort":"Why are most of us right-handed?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Right-handed people are dominant worldwide  but why? Jason G Goldman investigates.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Right-handed people are dominant worldwide  but why? Jason G Goldman investigates.","SummaryShort":"A vexing mystery of the human body","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-12-16T02:06:45Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"2fbbd77d-fe2a-455e-8f4c-c423b01a5d60","Id":"wwfuture/story/20141215-why-are-most-of-us-right-handed","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-12-16T02:06:45Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20141215-why-are-most-of-us-right-handed"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20141215-why-are-most-of-us-right-handed","_id":"559a56480b808738c4e2fa58"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Mid-conversation with Robert Provine, I have a compelling urge, rising from deep inside my body. The more I try to quash it, the more it seems to spread, until it consumes my whole being. Eventually, it is all I can think about &ndash; but how can I stop myself from yawning?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Provine tells me this often happens when people are talking to him; during presentations, he sometimes finds the majority of his audience with their mouths agape and tonsils swinging. Luckily, as a psychologist at the University of Maryland, Baltimore County and author of Curious Behavior: Yawning, Laughing, Hiccupping, and Beyond, he isn&rsquo;t offended. &ldquo;It makes a very effective lecture,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;You talk and then the audience starts yawning. And then you can ask people to experiment on their yawns &ndash; like closing the lips, or inhaling through clenched teeth, or trying to yawn with the nose pinched closed.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It is through experiments like these that Provine has tried to explore a millennia-old mystery: why do we yawn? We all know that tiredness, boredom, or the sight of someone else can all bring along the almost irrepressible urge &ndash; but what purpose does it serve the body? &nbsp;When he first started work on so-called &ldquo;chasmology&rdquo; in the late 80s, Provine wrote that &ldquo;yawning may have the dubious distinction of being the least understood, common human behaviour&rdquo;. Nearly three decades later, we may be closer to an answer, but it&rsquo;s one that has split the field.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p024lhzh\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Arguably the first studier of yawns was the Greek physician Hippocrates &lt;a href=\"http://www.karger.com/Book/Home/253772\"&gt;nearly 2,500 years ago&lt;/a&gt;. He believed that yawning helped to release noxious air, particularly during a fever. &ldquo;Like the large quantities of steam that escape from cauldrons when water boils, the accumulated air in the body is violently expelled through the mouth when the body temperature rises,&rdquo; he wrote. Different incarnations of the idea lingered until the 19th Century, when scientists instead proposed that yawning aids respiration &ndash; triggering a rush of oxygen into the blood supply, while flushing out the carbon dioxide. If that were true, you would expect people to yawn more or less frequently depending on the oxygen and carbon dioxide concentrations in the air. Yet when Provine asked volunteers to breathe various mixtures of gases, he found no such change.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Many theories have instead focussed on the strange, contagious nature of yawning &ndash; a fact that I know only too well from my conversation with Provine. &ldquo;Around 50% of people who observe a yawn will yawn in response,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;It is so contagious that anything associated with it will trigger one&hellip; seeing or hearing another person, or even reading about yawning.&rdquo; For this reason, some researchers have wondered if yawning might be a primitive form of communication &ndash; if so, what information is it transmitting? We often feel tired when we yawn, so one idea is that it helps set everyone&rsquo;s biological clocks to the same rhythm. &ldquo;In my view the most likely signalling role of yawning is to help to synchronize the behaviour of a social group &ndash; to make them go to sleep more or less at the same time,&rdquo; says Christian Hess, at the University of Bern in Switzerland. With the same routine, a group can then work together more efficiently throughout the day.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p024lj80\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet we also yawn during times of stress: &lt;a href=\"http://running.competitor.com/2014/01/training/making-the-case-for-the-pre-race-yawn_36250\"&gt;Olympic athletes often do it before a race&lt;/a&gt;, while musicians sometimes succumb before a concert. So some researchers, including Provine, believe that the strenuous movements might have a more general role in rebooting the brain &ndash; when you are sleepy they make you more alert, or when you are distracted they make you more focussed. Spreading through a group, contagious yawns could then help everyone reach the same level of attention, making them more vigilant to a threat, for instance. The mechanism is somewhat hazy &ndash; though one French researcher, Olivier Walusinski, proposes that yawning helps to &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/23813685\"&gt;pump cerebrospinal fluid around the brain&lt;/a&gt;, which could trigger a shift in neural activity.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;With so many competing and contradictory ideas, a grand unifying theory of yawning may seem like a distant speck on the horizon. But over the last few years, one underlying mechanism has emerged that could, potentially, appease all these apparent paradoxes in one fell swoop. Andrew Gallup, now at the State University of New York at Oneonta, was first inspired with the idea during his undergraduate degree, when he realised that &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3534187/\"&gt;yawning might help to chill the brain&lt;/a&gt; and stop it overheating. The violent movement of the jaws moves blood flow around the skull, he argued, helping to carry away excess heat, while the deep inhalation brings cool air into the sinus cavities and around the carotid artery leading back into the brain. What&rsquo;s more, the strenuous movements could also flex the membranes of sinuses &ndash; fanning a soft breeze through the cavities that should cause our mucus to evaporate, which should chill the head like air conditioning.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The most obvious test was to see if people are &lt;a href=\"http://www.epjournal.net/wp-content/uploads/ep0592101.pdf\"&gt;more or less likely to yawn in different temperatures&lt;/a&gt;. In normal conditions, Gallup found that around 48% felt the urge to yawn, but when he asked them to hold a cold compress to their foreheads, just 9% succumbed. Breathing through the nose, which could also cool the brain, was even more effective, completely dampening his subjects&rsquo; urge to yawn &ndash; potentially suggesting a handy trick for anyone facing embarrassment during a tedious conversation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p024mgqj\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Perhaps the best evidence comes from two troubled women who approached Gallup soon after he first published his results. Both were looking for relief from &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19657685\"&gt;pathological yawning attacks, sometimes lasting an hour at a time&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;It was extremely debilitating and interferes with any basic activity,&rdquo; says Gallup. &ldquo;They&rsquo;d have to walk away and go to a secluded area &ndash; it affected their personal and professional lives.&rdquo; Intriguingly, one of the women found the only way to stop the yawning attack was to throw herself into cold water. Inspired, Gallup asked them to place a thermometer in their mouths before and after the attacks. Sure enough, he saw a slight rise in temperature just before the yawning bouts, which continued until it dropped back to 37C.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Importantly, this brain chill might underlie the many, seemingly contradictory, events that lead to yawning. Our body temperature naturally rises before and after sleep, for instance. Cooling the brain slightly might also make us more alert &ndash; waking us up when we are bored and distracted. And by spreading from person to person, contagious yawns could therefore help a whole group to focus.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p024ljrm\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Gallup&rsquo;s unified theory has been somewhat contentious among yawning researchers. &ldquo;Gallup&rsquo;s group has failed to present any convincing experimental evidence to support his theory,&rdquo; says Hess. In particular, his critics point out he hasn&rsquo;t made direct measurements of temperature changes in the human brain, though Gallup says he has found the expected fluctuations in yawning rats. Provine is more positive, however &ndash; believing that it could be one way in which yawning helps the brain change state, and focus.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if Gallup has managed to find that unified theory, many mysteries remain. Why do foetuses yawn in the womb, for instance?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"magnify\":{ \"id\": \"Fetuses-Yawn-in-Utero-|-Video\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It could just be that they are practicing for life outside, or perhaps the yawn plays a more active role in guiding the body&rsquo;s growth &ndash; by helping to develop articulation in the jaws joints, for instance, or by encouraging the growth of the lungs, says Provine. If so, Provine suggests that yawning&rsquo;s functions in the womb may be more important than our attacks as adults.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Provine also points out that yawning &ndash; and perhaps other bodily functions, like sneezing &ndash; shares some strange parallels with sex. The facial expressions involved are surprisingly similar, he says &ndash; just take a look at this picture and you can see where he&rsquo;s coming from.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p024mhdk\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Like sex, yawns and sneezes involve a build-up that ends in a pleasant climax. &ldquo;Once initiated, they go to completion &ndash; you don&rsquo;t want a yawnus interruptus,&rdquo; is how Provine puts it. For these reasons, he wonders if a shared neural machinery underlying these different feelings. &ldquo;Mother Nature does not reinvent the wheel,&rdquo; he says. As evidence, he points to the fact that &lt;a href=\"http://baillement.com/clomipramine.html\"&gt;certain anti-depressants can lead some patients to orgasm during a yawn&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; a rare side effect that could quickly lose its appeal.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Eventually, the temptation to yawn just proved too irresistible during my conversation with Provine. It was a warm summer day, so perhaps my yawns were stopping my brain from over-heating during our stimulating conversation. Whatever function it was serving, the relief was almost worth the agonising wait.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I&rsquo;m willing to bet you&rsquo;ve been stifling a few yawns yourself by this point &ndash; as Provine points out, reading or even thinking about yawning can be enough to set us off. So go ahead, let it out &ndash; and do so in the knowledge that you are enjoying one of life&rsquo;s most enduring mysteries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-08-12T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"One of science's most baffling questions? Why we yawn","HeadlineShort":"Why do we yawn?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Yawning has puzzled scientists for more than two millennia. But could a new theory settle the question once and for all? David Robson investigates.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Yawning has puzzled scientists for more than two millennia. But could a new theory settle the question once and for all? David Robson investigates.","SummaryShort":"One of science's most baffling mysteries","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"b904e6de-c53a-4f7a-8097-463425112a0d","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140811-warning-this-may-make-you-yawn","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140811-warning-this-may-make-you-yawn"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140811-warning-this-may-make-you-yawn","_id":"559a563b0b808738c4e2f8e9"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s easy to spot the girls&rsquo; section of a children&rsquo;s clothes shop because most of it is pink. I know a lot of parents who insist that they would like their daughters to wear something different, but pink seems to hold an irresistible allure for them. But is that really true? Is it inevitable that girls are born to grow up to prefer pink? &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Various studies have looked at colour preferences in different age groups. In the US most have found that babies and toddlers, whether male or female, are attracted to primary colours such as red and blue. Pink doesn&rsquo;t feature high on the list, although it is more popular than brown and grey. &nbsp;Some studies of this age group have found blue is favoured, others red, but they rarely find any gender difference.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02c5b19\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2007, research conducted at Newcastle University in the UK asked &lt;a href=\"http://www.cell.com/current-biology/abstract/S0960-9822(07)01559-X\"&gt;adults for their favourite colour&lt;/a&gt;. Did most of the women choose pink, or even red? No. The colour which came out top, for both men and women, was blue. But women, on average, rated the reddish shades more highly than the men did. The authors speculated that this was because hunter-gatherer women traditionally had the job of collecting fruit, so they might be more attuned to reddish shades of berries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s not quite clear why this should influence their likes and dislikes. Perhaps it might lead to improved skills in discrimination between different shades of red, but there&rsquo;s a missing step in the logic. Since some red berries are delicious and others are poisonous, why should that make red your favourite colour? If women evolved to prefer red, this should be universal, but a study conducted last year with the Himba people in Namibia found there was &lt;a href=\"http://psycnet.apa.org/psycinfo/2012-30151-001/\"&gt;no preference for reddish tones&lt;/a&gt; among women. &lt;br /&gt; &lt;strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Hard-wired colours?&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Cultural norms may also shape colour preferences. In cultures where pink is considered the appropriate colour for a baby girl and blue for a baby boy, babies become accustomed from birth to spending time wearing or even surrounded by, those colours. This makes it hard to know whether any preferences expressed later on are hard-wired. But a study from 2011 tried to get closer to discovering what&rsquo;s going on.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When one-year-old girls and boys were shown pairs of identical objects such as bracelets, pill boxes and picture frames, but with one object pink and another of a second colour, they were &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.2044-835X.2011.02027.x/abstract\"&gt;no more likely to choose pink than any other colour&lt;/a&gt;. But after the age of two the girls started to like pink and, by four, boys were determined in their rejection of pink. This is the precise time when toddlers start to become aware of their gender, to talk about it and even to look around them to see what defines boy and what defines a girl. But just like adults, even very small children show biases towards their own group.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This group bias was also seen another study where three-to-five-year-olds were given &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16942493\"&gt;red or blue t-shirts to wear at nursery&lt;/a&gt;. For one group, the red and blue t-shirts were constantly referred to, and by the end of three weeks the children liked everything about their own colour group better. And that was just three weeks. Gender becomes a key topic of discussion from early pregnancy onwards. When we hear the news of the birth of a new baby, there&rsquo;s just one thing we want to know. Is it a boy or a girl?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02c5b38\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You could argue that it doesn&rsquo;t really matter what colour babies are exposed to the most, but it can even affect the way we, as adults, treat them. There&rsquo;s one famous study showing that women treated the exact same babies differently &lt;a href=\"http://www.jstor.org/discover/10.2307/1128775?uid=3738032&amp;uid=2129&amp;uid=2&amp;uid=70&amp;uid=4&amp;sid=21104447047971\"&gt;depending on whether they were dressed in pink or blue&lt;/a&gt;. If the clothes were blue they assumed it was a boy, played more physical games with them and encouraged them to play with a squeaky hammer, whereas they would gently soothe the baby dressed in pink and choose a doll for them to play with.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Pink for boys?&lt;/strong&gt; &lt;span&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/span&gt;But what about the idea that a century ago little boys were dressed in pink and pink for girls is only a recent fashion? It seems even that might be something of a myth too. Psychology writer Christian Jarrett describes in his new book Great Myths of the Brain, how an Italian psychologist Marco Del Giudice, who &lt;a href=\"http://www.researchgate.net/publication/229437155_The_Twentieth_Century_Reversal_of_Pink-Blue_Gender_Coding_A_Scientific_Urban_Legend\"&gt;tried to find the origins of this idea&lt;/a&gt;, could find just four short magazine quotes, describing pink as the colour for boys. In two of these he believes that perhaps the blue and pink were accidentally swapped around. That seems unlikely to me, but when he searched a database of five million books printed in American or British English from 1800-2000 more convincing was the lack of any mentions of &ldquo;pink for a boy&rdquo;, even though from 1890 onwards there were increasing mentions of &ldquo;pink for a girl&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even the association of pink with femininity today can backfire if it&rsquo;s not used in the right way. Pink is often used for breast cancer campaigns, but researchers at Erasmus University Rotterdam found that when &lt;a href=\"http://journals.ama.org/doi/abs/10.1509/jmkr.48.3.413\"&gt;women were shown adverts dominated by the colour pink&lt;/a&gt;, they were in fact less likely to think they&rsquo;d contract breast cancer themselves or to donate money to a cancer charity. The authors don&rsquo;t believe this was because they hated the colour pink, but because when they were reminded of their gender so overtly, the adverts felt so personally threatening that it set off denial mechanisms. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But there is one way at least in which pink can be useful for both women and men. Back in 2002 researchers in Switzerland who were keen &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/12026752\"&gt;to increase the response rate to surveys&lt;/a&gt;, found that printing questionnaires on coloured paper made no difference, unless the paper was pink, in which case 12% more people filled it in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Colours, it seems, influence our behaviour much more than we realise.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;You can hear more Medical Myths on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p002vsyw\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Health Check on the BBC World Service&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Disclaimer&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;All content within this column is provided for general information only, and should not be treated as a substitute for the medical advice of your own doctor or any other health care professional.              <div class="primary-header-wrapper">
              <div class="primary-header primary-header-with-context">

               </ul>
               <h1 class="primary-heading" role="heading">
                How your face betrays your personality and health
               </h1>
              </div>
             </div>
             <div class="secondary-header-wrapper">
              <div aria-hidden="true" class="bbccom_slot" data-advert-groups="4">
               <div class="bbccom_advert bbccom_display_none">
                <script type="text/javascript">
                 /*&lt;![CDATA[*/
                                if (window.bbcdotcom &amp;&amp; bbcdotcom.slot) {

                                }
                            /*]]&gt;*/
                </script>
               </div>
               <script type="text/javascript">
                /*&lt;![CDATA[*/
                        if (window.bbcdotcom &amp;&amp; bbcdotcom.show) {

                        }
                        /*]]&gt;*/
                    &lt;/script&gt;
                &lt;/div&gt;

                        
                        



        

                    &lt;/div&gt;
                &lt;/div&gt;
            &lt;/div&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
        &lt;!-- hero-unit end --&gt;
        &lt;div id="story-content" class="page-component-wrapper standard article-content"&gt;

            &lt;!-- primary content start --&gt;
            &lt;div class="primary-content"  role="main"&gt;
                        &lt;div class="mpu-wrapper"&gt;
            
    &lt;div data-advert-groups="1_2_3"  class="bbccom_slot" aria-hidden="true"&gt;
        &lt;div class="bbccom_advert bbccom_display_none"&gt;
            &lt;script type="text/javascript"&gt;
                /*&lt;![CDATA[*/
                    if (window.bbcdotcom &amp;&amp; bbcdotcom.slot) {
                        bbcdotcom.slot('mpu', [1,2,3]);

                /*]]&gt;*/

              </div>
              <script type="text/javascript">

            }
            /*]]&gt;*/
              </script>
             </div>
            </div>
            <div aria-hidden="true" class="bbccom_slot" data-advert-groups="1_2_3">
             <div class="bbccom_advert bbccom_display_none">
              <script type="text/javascript">
